> Does anybody has some documents about sql in cobol ?
> 
> thx

You are more likely to get helpful responses if you specify which
versions of SQL and COBOl and what operating system and hardware you
are using or thinking of using.

The link to the COBOL FAQ regularly posted on this group contains a
list of compiler providers and their websites, many/most/all of them
provide documentation on their websites.

Without being exhaustive SQL suppliers are

IBM
ORACLE
MICROSOFT
INFORMIX
Microfocus (probably)
MYSQL

Again they all have websites and some if not all have the
documentation available there.

Robert
```

```

"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
news:joe_zitzelberger-CF4A7C.22093923032004@corp.supernews.com...

> Still, if a user is working at the Cobol level, they have already
> indicated a lack of concern with how the object code is going to be
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> they use an assembler?

What *assembler* would you suggest using?

The majority of the system software is written in one or another dialect of
ALGOL, which works very well for system software but works rather less well
for things like numeric-edited data items, data records, and indexed files,
and even less well for computations involving implicit decimal points in
DISPLAY or PACKED-DECIMAL form.  And there is even some *system* software
written in one or another dialect of COBOL.

But there is no software on Unisys MCP systems written in assembler because
there is no assembler (nor has there been in the four-decade-plus history of
the architecture), as I indicated elsewhere.

What assembler would you suggest the programmer use on that system?

    -Chuck Stevens
```

 "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:

> "Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
> news:joe_zitzelberger-CF4A7C.22093923032004@corp.supernews.com...
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> 

I wouldn't suggest using one if one is not available for the machine.  I 
suggest that someone working at the Cobol level need not know or 
understand the underlying memory architecture.

Making the actual source 'aware' of the need for segmentation is an odd 
and unusual approach, especially in a portable language like Cobol.  
Most compilers seem to handle that type of thing with compiler commands 
(pragmas, {$SEGM xx}, etc), not actual source alterations.

Not only does it make your Cobol non-portable, as the next machines 
compiler might puke on your extra labels violating its segmentation  
style.  It also requires Cobol programmers to understand 
segments/overlays/memory arch/etc, something they are not usually 
willing, or even capible of doing.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Perfrom Thru

_(thread depth: 23)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-04-06T16:43:19-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c4vfao$2m9f$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<49f7d61d.0403142339.1c3c17df@posting.google.com> <c4rvj2$aus$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <joe_zitzelberger-DB9AF5.03290606042004@corp.supernews.com> <96N5j6peflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <PLycc.17219$Dv2.4101@newsread2.news.pas.earthlink.net> <tt16705tim7vdeuqq07vo85m760g4f9st1@4ax.com>`

```
According to the segmentation module in both ANSI X3.23-1974 and ANSI
X3.23-1985, as I understand the writeup:     all SECTIONs with a
segment-number from 1 to 49 inclusive are some form of "permanent segments"
or "fixed overlayable segments", and all of those from 50 to 99 inclusive
are "independent segments".

Aside from *restrictions* on PERFORM flow and on SORT and MERGE input or
output procedures, the only behavioral difference I'm aware of is whether or
not ALTERed GO TO's in "independent segments" are in their "last-altered"
state or their "initial" state when the SECTION is entered.

In '85 COBOL, both ALTER and section-numbers are deprecated, and in
COBOL2002, they're gone.  Without ALTER, section-numbers have no functional
utility, as I see it.

What do you propose to do with them?

    -Chuck Stevens

"Paul Knudsen" <HughG@dodgeit.com> wrote in message
news:tt16705tim7vdeuqq07vo85m760g4f9st1@4ax.com...

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Memory management (was: Perfrom Thru

_(thread depth: 25)_

- **From:** "William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com>
- **Date:** 2004-04-14T02:05:54+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<601fc.8778$k05.7704@newsread2.news.pas.earthlink.net>`
- **References:** `<49f7d61d.0403142339.1c3c17df@posting.google.com> <c4rvj2$aus$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <joe_zitzelberger-DB9AF5.03290606042004@corp.supernews.com> <96N5j6peflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <PLycc.17219$Dv2.4101@newsread2.news.pas.earthlink.net> <tt16705tim7vdeuqq07vo85m760g4f9st1@4ax.com> <c4vfao$2m9f$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <mr6p70hvpusjv3t9mg7fck68b3c1vfvfj7@4ax.com>`

```
Interestingly enough, even when the Standard *did* include a "segmentation"
module, it made not implicit or explicit statements about "loading" or not
loading specific segments.  It certainly ALLOWED for code not to be "loaded into
memory" - but it made no such requirement.

This is similar to a (misconception) about the CANCEL statement.  There is
nothing in the Standard that says that a CANCEL statement must "free" from
memory the storage of a subprogram.

The Standard does talk about the SEMANTICS of CANCEL (and previously
segmentation) but these can be fully met with or without ever doing "memory
management".
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Memory management (was: Perfrom Thru

_(thread depth: 26)_

- **From:** Paul Knudsen <HughG@dodgeit.com>
- **Date:** 2004-04-15T02:10:51+00:00

```

"Paul Knudsen" <HughG@dodgeit.com> wrote in message
news:mr6p70hvpusjv3t9mg7fck68b3c1vfvfj7@4ax.com...
> On Tue, 6 Apr 2004 16:43:19 -0700, "Chuck Stevens"
> <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:
>
> >In '85 COBOL, both ALTER and section-numbers are deprecated, and in
> >COBOL2002, they're gone.  Without ALTER, section-numbers have no
functional
> >utility, as I see it.
> >
> >What do you propose to do with them?
>
> To only load rarely run code when it is needed.

On Unisys MCP systems, *no* code segment is loaded until it is needed, and
if it's rarely "touched" it's rarely loaded to begin with.  That's true
whether the code segmentation schemata have anything to do with the SECTION
and paragraph structure of the PROCEDURE DIVISION (as they do for Unisys MCP
COBOL74) or not (as is the case for Unisys MCP COBOL85).  Support for the
state of ALTERed GO TO's within permanent and temporary SECTIONs in COBOL85
is handled orthogonally to code segmentation.

    -Chuck Stevens

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c52154$1bo3$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<49f7d61d.0403142339.1c3c17df@posting.google.com> <c4rvj2$aus$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <joe_zitzelberger-DB9AF5.03290606042004@corp.supernews.com> <96N5j6peflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <joe_zitzelberger-0E96A5.09100207042004@corp.supernews.com>`

```
"Joe Zitzelberger" <joe_zitzelberger@nospam.com> wrote in message
news:joe_zitzelberger-0E96A5.09100207042004@corp.supernews.com...

> If the compiler in question was using the Cobol style segmentation of
> SECTIONs with PRIORITYs I wouldn't question it.
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> juggle labels to get your segments right.  That strikes me as freaky.

Sounds like "contempt prior to investigation".

How many source lines do you think it is *appropriate* for a programmer to
include in a single paragraph before the maintainability of the paragraph
degrades?   I was advised a quarter of a century ago anything more than
sixty or so was pushing the envelope at best, and like as not evidence that
the author was writing clever and obscure code for the sole purpose of
writing clever and obscure code without any view toward anything but his own
self-satisfaction and his feeble efforts at job security.  I haven't seen
any evidence that super-long paragraphs were now a Good Thing as far as
COBOL style was concerned.

So long as you don't have extremely large paragraphs, you're not going to
encounter the problem except in VERY rare circumstances.  The most recent
one I encountered was several hundred tests of multi-valued-and-ranged
88-level items in a single paragraph, which blew the paragraph limit when
the programmer increased the size of the "parent" data item for the 88's
from three bytes to eight (without, I might add, simultaneously adjusting
the 88's values!).

Moreover, the compiler in question is no longer being enhanced.  Unisys
believes it works adequately in this particular area.  The "boundary
conditions" in which it requires source change are infrequently encountered.
If you feel so strongly that this design has cooties, I'm sure Unisys would
respond to an offer on your part to cover the costs of modifying the MCP
COBOL74 compiler to provide a "borderless segmentation" scheme (such as MCP
COBOL85 already has!).

But as it stands right now, we've got about thirty-five years of history
indicating that those who object to this approach are few and those who have
supported it (in both the original (COBOL(68) compiler as well as the
COBOL74 compiler still supported today) many, particularly since neither the
Segmentation Module nor the ALTER verb is in any way deprecated by the
standards that are, or were, applicable to them.

      -Chuck Stevens
```

This thread reminds me of the UNIVAC 60 COBOL
Compiler.  First release:

Go to depending on dataname.

(forget what ever rules were specified, as there were few then)

The comiler designer(s) reconded that dataname would
be the equivalent of A thru Z in one form or another, and
allowed for 26 "go to dependings". In those days it was
not unheard of to consider values controling this feature
ranging to the size of a table or other control. One hundred, perhaps. More
if needed.

Code in compiler was changed. Don't know if anyone
ever had to deal with the limitation or used the extended
range. I would be surprised if any compiler could avoid
setting limits to some variables. The standard does not
set one today, does it?

Warren

Standard in the same manner as exceeding the compiler's limits.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Perfrom Thru

_(thread depth: 26)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-04-08T09:51:43-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c53vuv$2mjt$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<49f7d61d.0403142339.1c3c17df@posting.google.com> <c52154$1bo3$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <c525sh$jhi$1@panix1.panix.com> <QR1dc.9605$Po2.3657486@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net> <c53h07$6ht$1@panix5.panix.com>`

```
In ANSI X3.23-1974, on page I-6, in section 1.5, DEFINITION OF AN
IMPLEMENTATION OF AMERICAN NATIONAL STANDARD COBOL, paragraph (4), I find:

"In general, the American National Standard COBOL specification specifies no
upper limit on such things as the number of statements in a program, the
number of operands permitted in certain statements, etc.  It is recognized
that these limits will vary from one implementation of American National
Standard COBOL to another and may prevent compilation of some programs that
meet the requirements of this standard."

I note with interest the constructs "such things as", "etc.", "may",  and
"some" as used in this citation.

I believe it is clear from the above that while the compiler may
successfully produce object code for a single instance of some particular
statement, it is not obligated to produce object code if, say, the
programmer attempts a billion replications of it in the Procedure Division
of a single program -- whether those billion replications are grouped into
multiple paragraphs or not.

In order to resolve this issue, if the implementor has properly documented
such limits and the appropriate approach to avoiding them, it is clearly
incumbent on the programmer to avoid them rather than demanding that the
limit be eliminated by the compiler implementor.

Stated another way, I see nothing in the above citation that the compiler
implementor is required to provide an entirely separate mechanism and an
entirely separate identification scheme to "prevent the proper compilation
of ... programs" when such limits as described in the citation are
encountered, from those which are used when some explicit rule in the
standard is violated.

In this particular case, the compiler issues
    ERROR 008:  THE CODE SEGMENT SIZE LIMIT (8191) HAS BEEN EXCEEDED.
at the location at which the code segment overflow occurs.  It is identified
there as an ERROR, not as a SYNTAX ERROR.

At the end of compilation, presuming a listing has been produced, the
compilation summary includes
    DID NOT COMPILE.
    NUMBER OF ERRORS DETECTED: <total number of errors>
        LAST ERROR AT <sequence number>

The term SYNTAX ERROR doesn't appear in the compilation summary either.

The same is true for any other syntax error, be it "This cannot start in
Margin A", "Only subordinate items are allowed after an 'OCCURS DEPENDING'
clause", or even "At least 3 and at most 8 SORT tapes are allowed" (this
last representing limits on a Unisys extension carried over from COBOL(68),
and almost identical to a similar extension described in my copy of IBM DOS
COBOL from 1973, that doesn't identify the construct being diagnosed as an
extension to ANSI X3.23-1968).

The compiler does not distinguish  *categorically* between violations of
ANSI standard syntax that are not otherwise covered by extensions and
violations of implementor-defined limits as described in the aforementioned
citations.  They are both errors that prevent the proper compilation of the
program containing the offending statement or construct.  The compiler
identifies them as such to the user, and notifies the operating environment
that the compiler has prevented the proper compilation of the program
accordingly.

Having encountered such an error, the compiler has two basic choices, I
think:

1)  It can prevent the successful compilation of the program, identify the
offending statement, and either through the error itself or through the
documentation indicate what's wrong with it and how to fix it, while
attempting to see if there's anything else wrong with the rest of the
program.

2)  It can fall over dead on the spot with a program dump of the compiler
and an indication along the lines of SEG ARRAY ERROR, FALSE ASSERTION,
INVALID OP, INV INDX, etc..  Since most of our customers don't purchase the
source to those products for which it is available, a program dump of a
COBOL compiler is not a whole lot of use in helping the user figure out
what's wrong with his program and what to do about it.

There are three possible termination modes for a compilation:  "I went to a
normal EOJ as a program but could not produce, or was enjoined from
producing, object code as a compiler" is identified by the operating
environment as "SNTX".  As successful compilation is recorded as "EOT" (end
of task).  The equivalent of "I fell over dead (whether through an aneurism
or a bullet)" is some category of "-DS" -- e.g., "O-DS" for "somebody shot
me", "F-DS" for "I had an aneurism", "P-DS" for "I committed suicide", etc.
These are the choices for compilers in that environment, whether they have
standards defining what the compiler should explicitly identify as a "Syntax
Error" or not.

I don't find any indication in ANSI X3.23-1974 that the implementor is
required to differentiate *categorically* between cases in which rules
explicitly set forth in the standard are violated in a way that would
prevent the proper compilation of the program, and cases in which rules
imposed by the implementor in explicit conformance with the above citation
are violated in a way that would prevent the proper compilation of the
program.

In general, we try to turn all involuntary cases of approach (2) into
approach (1) (there's not much the compiler can do if the user shoots the
compilation task down), but even in this quarter-century-and-more-old
product the occasional program fault does occur.

But given this citation from the '74 standard, I'm at a loss to understand
why you feel the standard requires the compiler to notify the user of errors
like "your paragraph is too big" using terminology or mechanisms entirely
separate from those that are used with the likes of "write a record, read a
file".  Both of these fall into the "I went to a normal EOJ as a program but
could not produce object code" category, it seems to me, and the other
alternatives do not strike me as useful.

I find the citation from ANSI X3.23-1974 abundantly clear.  I am not
convinced that the fact that it seems not to be so clear to you remains my
problem to solve.

    -Chuck Stevens

> Warren Simmons <wsimmons5@optonline.net> wrote:
…[19 more quoted lines elided]…
>
```

From:

<http://groups.google.com/groups?selm=c39vmh%242kr%241%40si05.rsvl.unisys.com&oe=UTF-8&output=gplain>

--begin quoted text:

I once saw a complaint from a user that changing the DATA DIVISION code
caused a Code Segment Limit syntax error in Procedure Division code.

Perhaps an accurate reporting of this message, Mr Stevens, rather than the 
description above, might have saved some very busy people a great deal of 
very precious time.  Care, competence, diligence and professional 
discipline might, in the future, prevent such an occurrence.

DD

```
It is also worth noting that the '74 Standard (as well as the '68 and even the
'85 Standard) has *NO* requirement that a compiler issue ANY specific errors
against "invalid syntax".  This requirement was added in the 2002 Standard.

A conforming compiler (before the 2002 Standard) could simply issue a final
message saying

  "can't compile this program"

If ANYTHING stopped it from compiling ("syntax error" - Limitation violation -
other)
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Perfrom Thru

_(thread depth: 28)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-04-08T16:28:24-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c54n6p$4hs$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<49f7d61d.0403142339.1c3c17df@posting.google.com> <c52154$1bo3$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <c525sh$jhi$1@panix1.panix.com> <QR1dc.9605$Po2.3657486@news4.srv.hcvlny.cv.net> <c53h07$6ht$1@panix5.panix.com> <c53vuv$2mjt$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <S0ldc.2160$A_4.1882@newsread1.news.pas.earthlink.net>`

```
I'm reminded of the single compiler I've ever run across that DID
distinguish fundamentally between a syntax error and a limit error:  a
resident BASIC compiler/interpreter for the Interdata Model 3 minicomputer.

If the program was syntactically incorrect in some fashion, the software
would print
    ?
on the teletype.  Most of the time it would print the line of source that
encountered the problem.

If the program encountered some sort of limit, it would print
    !
on the teletype; rarely would it give you an accurate indication of what the
nature of the overflow was or where it occurred.  It could be anything from
exponent overflow to divide by zero to too much program for the memory
available after loading the compiler/interpreter.  Most often it was the
last, in our case.

We didn't use the interpreter much, and as the available FORTRAN compiler
was for the Interdata Model 4 which had hardware floating-point (our 3
didn't even have multiply/divide), we opted for assembler language.

    -Chuck Stevens

"William M. Klein" <wmklein@nospam.netcom.com> wrote in message
news:S0ldc.2160$A_4.1882@newsread1.news.pas.earthlink.net...

…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> > IMPLEMENTATION OF AMERICAN NATIONAL STANDARD COBOL, paragraph (4), I
find:
> >
> > "In general, the American National Standard COBOL specification
specifies no
> > upper limit on such things as the number of statements in a program, the
> > number of operands permitted in certain statements, etc.  It is
recognized
> > that these limits will vary from one implementation of American National
> > Standard COBOL to another and may prevent compilation of some programs
that
> > meet the requirements of this standard."
> >
> > I note with interest the constructs "such things as", "etc.", "may",
and
> > "some" as used in this citation.
> >
> > I believe it is clear from the above that while the compiler may
> > successfully produce object code for a single instance of some
particular
> > statement, it is not obligated to produce object code if, say, the
> > programmer attempts a billion replications of it in the Procedure
Division
> > of a single program -- whether those billion replications are grouped
into
> > multiple paragraphs or not.
> >
> > In order to resolve this issue, if the implementor has properly
documented
> > such limits and the appropriate approach to avoiding them, it is clearly
> > incumbent on the programmer to avoid them rather than demanding that the
> > limit be eliminated by the compiler implementor.
> >
> > Stated another way, I see nothing in the above citation that the
compiler
> > implementor is required to provide an entirely separate mechanism and an
> > entirely separate identification scheme to "prevent the proper
compilation
> > of ... programs" when such limits as described in the citation are
> > encountered, from those which are used when some explicit rule in the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> > at the location at which the code segment overflow occurs.  It is
identified
> > there as an ERROR, not as a SYNTAX ERROR.
> >
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> > Margin A", "Only subordinate items are allowed after an 'OCCURS
DEPENDING'
> > clause", or even "At least 3 and at most 8 SORT tapes are allowed" (this
> > last representing limits on a Unisys extension carried over from
COBOL(68),
> > and almost identical to a similar extension described in my copy of IBM
DOS
> > COBOL from 1973, that doesn't identify the construct being diagnosed as
an
> > extension to ANSI X3.23-1968).
> >
> > The compiler does not distinguish  *categorically* between violations of
> > ANSI standard syntax that are not otherwise covered by extensions and
> > violations of implementor-defined limits as described in the
aforementioned
> > citations.  They are both errors that prevent the proper compilation of
the
> > program containing the offending statement or construct.  The compiler
> > identifies them as such to the user, and notifies the operating
environment
> > that the compiler has prevented the proper compilation of the program
> > accordingly.
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> > 1)  It can prevent the successful compilation of the program, identify
the
> > offending statement, and either through the error itself or through the
> > documentation indicate what's wrong with it and how to fix it, while
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> > 2)  It can fall over dead on the spot with a program dump of the
compiler
> > and an indication along the lines of SEG ARRAY ERROR, FALSE ASSERTION,
> > INVALID OP, INV INDX, etc..  Since most of our customers don't purchase
the
> > source to those products for which it is available, a program dump of a
> > COBOL compiler is not a whole lot of use in helping the user figure out
> > what's wrong with his program and what to do about it.
> >
> > There are three possible termination modes for a compilation:  "I went
to a
> > normal EOJ as a program but could not produce, or was enjoined from
> > producing, object code as a compiler" is identified by the operating
> > environment as "SNTX".  As successful compilation is recorded as "EOT"
(end
> > of task).  The equivalent of "I fell over dead (whether through an
aneurism
> > or a bullet)" is some category of "-DS" -- e.g., "O-DS" for "somebody
shot
> > me", "F-DS" for "I had an aneurism", "P-DS" for "I committed suicide",
etc.
> > These are the choices for compilers in that environment, whether they
have
> > standards defining what the compiler should explicitly identify as a
"Syntax
> > Error" or not.
> >
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> > imposed by the implementor in explicit conformance with the above
citation
> > are violated in a way that would prevent the proper compilation of the
> > program.
> >
> > In general, we try to turn all involuntary cases of approach (2) into
> > approach (1) (there's not much the compiler can do if the user shoots
the
> > compilation task down), but even in this quarter-century-and-more-old
> > product the occasional program fault does occur.
> >
> > But given this citation from the '74 standard, I'm at a loss to
understand
> > why you feel the standard requires the compiler to notify the user of
errors
> > like "your paragraph is too big" using terminology or mechanisms
entirely
> > separate from those that are used with the likes of "write a record,
read a
> > file".  Both of these fall into the "I went to a normal EOJ as a program
but
> > could not produce object code" category, it seems to me, and the other
> > alternatives do not strike me as useful.
> >
> > I find the citation from ANSI X3.23-1974 abundantly clear.  I am not
> > convinced that the fact that it seems not to be so clear to you remains
my
> > problem to solve.

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On  7-Apr-2004, "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com> wrote:
> 
> It shouldn't be the compiler deciding the answer to this.
...
> Excellent guideline.   However it should just be a guideline.   It is quite
> possible to envision a very clear 600 line evaluate statement that matches a
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
> WHEN OTHER PERFORM-REST-OF-THE-EVALUATE

This misspelled thread is dealing with a 30-YEAR-OLD PRODUCT from 
Unisys!  If I were a Unisys techie, and someone complained about this, I 
would recommend that they try using our COBOL 85 product instead.  Very 
few items were actually removed from COBOL 85 that were present in COBOL 
74, and replacing these with acceptable substitutes is a quite simple task.

I'm not trying to rehash the whole debate on what "it" (being the error 
in question) should be called.  However, I completely understand Chuck's 
answer that Unisys has no plans to correct this.  It's no different than 
  Microsoft saying they're no longer going to support Windows 98 - and 
that's only a 6-year-old product!

As to them "selling" the compiler, Unisys operating systems (at least 
the 2200 side, but I believe the A-series is like this too) come with 
what's called the Integrated Operating Environment (IOE).  This 
environment includes the operating system, compilers for various 
languages, database management systems, and support utilities.  They're 
not "selling" COBOL 74 compilers - they're merely including them in 
their mainframe sales.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Perfrom Thru

_(thread depth: 25)_

- **From:** "Chuck Stevens" <charles.stevens@unisys.com>
- **Date:** 2004-04-08T15:39:39-07:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c54kbb$2kv$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com>`
- **References:** `<49f7d61d.0403142339.1c3c17df@posting.google.com> <c4rvj2$aus$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <joe_zitzelberger-DB9AF5.03290606042004@corp.supernews.com> <96N5j6peflB@jpberlin-l.willms.jpberlin.de> <joe_zitzelberger-0E96A5.09100207042004@corp.supernews.com> <c52154$1bo3$1@si05.rsvl.unisys.com> <c53lto$t92$1@peabody.colorado.edu> <107bi3966vjg1b9@corp.supernews.com>`

```
"LX-i" <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message
news:107bi3966vjg1b9@corp.supernews.com...
> Howard Brazee wrote:

> This misspelled thread is dealing with a 30-YEAR-OLD PRODUCT from
> Unisys!  If I were a Unisys techie, and someone complained about this, I
> would recommend that they try using our COBOL 85 product instead.

Already did that in this thread.  Several times.  Not necessarily an
appropriate response to a customer contact or trouble report in this case
(though there are cases, for example, where customers want COBOL85's
"Explicit Library Declaration Interface" extension or some of the "Intrinsic
Function" module from ANSI-85, and the appropriate response does indeed
become a less-confrontational version of "If you want to enjoy the features
and functionality of Unisys MCP COBOL85, the appropriate product to use to
obtain them is not Unisys MCP COBOL74"!.

In this instance, COBOL85 exhibits the opposite behavior:  segment
boundaries don't have anything to do with paragraph-names or sections, and
several contributors to this thread have indicated they prefer that.  Fine.
They should use COBOL85, then.  It's true, you don't ever burst the limit on
code segment size.  But it is possible for a code segment transition to
occur in the middle of a very tight loop in a program, and there's not much
the programmer can do except shift the boundary (and even that requires that
he have some understanding of the layout of the generated object code!).
That's why I've indicated repeatedly that I'd like to see the user be able
to specify which mechanism he wishes to use (with the current COBOL85 scheme
remaining the default).

> Very few items were actually removed from COBOL 85 that were present in
> COBOL 74, and replacing these with acceptable substitutes is a quite
simple task.

Unisys also provides a migration tool that is designed to take care of the
"tweaks".  For example, "USAGE BINARY" was a Unisys extension in '74, but is
part of the standard in '85.  In '74, it didn't include the "odometer
effect", in '85 it does, so both COBOL74 and COBOL85 have syntax to provide
compatibility with each other (BINARY TRUNCATED in COBOL74 to match BINARY
[EXTENDED] in COBOL85, BINARY EXTENDED in COBOL85 to match BINARY
[TRUNCATED] in COBOL74).  There are many such "tweaks".  This product is
included at no cost on the COBOL85 product media.  (May also be on the
COBOL74 product media, though I'm not certain of that.  In any case, we give
it away just so people will be more inclined to migrate to COBOL85).

> As to them "selling" the compiler, Unisys operating systems (at least
> the 2200 side, but I believe the A-series is like this too) come with
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> their mainframe sales.

I don't think that's quite right for MCP systems, though I'm not on the
marketing side and don't have access to those details.  There are some
packages that include application development tools -- some of those may
include one application language compiler of the user's choice -- but my
recollection is that licenses for COBOL74 are still available; users who
want one really have to weork to get it (even though the license fees are
currently much lower than for COBOL85).  Also, we are not accepting user
requests for enhancements or new features for COBOL74, giving our users even
more incentive to migrate to COBOL85.

But, as was the case with COBOL(68) once COBOL74 was introduced, there are a
lot of very, very loyal users of COBOL74.  It is indeed a mature product,
and many users find no particular reason to migrate away from something that
has been working just fine for them for decades.  We began the process of
terminating support for COBOL(68) in the late 1980's, every compilation from
1992 onward included a warning that support for the compiler was going away,
it was finally discontinued as of 12/31/1999, and as late as last year we
still had customers using it under special support agreements.  So based on
this painful experience, I don't expect that COBOL74 will disappear
altogether any time in the near, or even foreseeable, future.

    -Chuck Stevens
```

- **References:** `<49f7d61d.0403142339.1c3c17df@posting.google.com> <106l3ao1f4ef8f6@corp.supernews.com> <c4e6ji$ljd$1@panix1.panix.com> <106m59bks0749ad@corp.supernews.com> <c4f7p6$iae$1@panix5.panix.com>`

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:c4f7p6$iae$1@panix5.panix.com...
> In article <106m59bks0749ad@corp.supernews.com>,
> Rick Smith <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
> >
> ><docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message
news:c4e6ji$ljd$1@panix1.panix.com...
> >> In article <106l3ao1f4ef8f6@corp.supernews.com>,
[snip]
> >> Meaning, as Wittgenstein tells it, is the result of interpretation, Mr
> >> Smith; my intention for this phrase relied on the definition for
'syntax'
> >> which - in the absence of any other - I supplied earlier in the thread.
> >
[snip]
> >
> >Wittgenstein was wrong! Meaning is as intended and the use of
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
> view.

< http://www.m-w.com/ > meaning : '1 a : the thing one intends to
convey especially by language'.

< http://www.m-w.com/ > interpret : '1 : to explain or tell the meaning
of : present in understandable terms'.

Antony Flew, "A Dictionary of Philosophy," interpretation : 'The
assigning of meanings to the expressions of a formal language.'

Mr Dwarf, from the common definitions, it would appear that
interpretation cannot occur until the meaning is known. That is,
how can the reader 'explain or tell the meaning of' without first
knowing 'the thing [the author] intends to convey'? Thus, meaning
cannot be the result of interpretation.

However, understanding that Wittgenstein was a philospher who
worked with languages and borrowing from Flew, I could find, by
interpretation, that Wittgenstein may have intended that '[the]
meaning [assigned to an expression] is the result of interpretation'.
Of course, if my interpretation is not what Wittgenstein intended,
then '[my] use of interpretation [would have led] to [a]
misunderstanding.'

On the other hand, if 'meaning is the result of interpretation' was
intended to explain how one decides 'the thing one intends to
convey', then the quote seems inappropriate for the context of
the prior discussion (interpretation of words). Within the context
of deciding 'the thing one intends to convey' it is not necessary
'to posit a world in which a thing-in-itself can be transmitted by
language' since a thing-for-others serves that purpose nicely.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT} Wittgenstein and Meaning (was Re: Perfrom Thru)

_(thread depth: 50)_

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com
- **Date:** 2004-04-15T09:24:18-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c5m2e2$m4o$1@panix5.panix.com>`
- **References:** `<49f7d61d.0403142339.1c3c17df@posting.google.com> <106m59bks0749ad@corp.supernews.com> <c4f7p6$iae$1@panix5.panix.com> <107spv3499u0j68@corp.supernews.com>`

```
In article <107spv3499u0j68@corp.supernews.com>,
Rick Smith <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
>
><docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:c4f7p6$iae$1@panix5.panix.com...
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
>assigning of meanings to the expressions of a formal language.'

L Wittgenstein: 'The meaning of a word is in its use'.

>
>Mr Dwarf, from the common definitions, it would appear that
>interpretation cannot occur until the meaning is known.

This might be a reason for my using Wittgenstein's technical definition 
instead of the common ones.  When I address how the speaker uses (applies) 
a word I refer to 'intention', when I address how a listener uses 
(applies) a word I speak of 'interpretation'... and I try to avoid 
'meaning' as imprecise because of situations like:

Person A: 'When I say (x) it means (y).'
Person B: 'No, when you say (x) it means (z).'

... as they both confer a 'meaning' which exists outside of use.  Consider 
the classic:

'Time flies like an arrow, fruit flies like a banana.'

What is the 'meaning' of 'fruit' here?  It may be intended one way and 
interpreted another; that is why I employ:

Person A; 'When I say (x) I intend (y).'
Person B: 'When you say (x) I interpret it as (z).'

... thus clearly delineating the language as a medium which is used by 
humans and not a representation of reified concepts.

DD
```

language's function of conveying, from one to another.

>Many years ago -- I don't recall the
>circumstances -- I found it necessary to conclude that words
>have definitions and that meaning consists in how words are
>used in context.

'... meaning consists on how words are used in context' appears to rather 
close to 'Meaning is in use', aye.

>instead of 'Smith', I knew what you meant though the definitions

You might have guessed my intention, Mr Smith, and you might even have 
been correct... this once.  As Wittgenstein asked on a different occaision 
- and here taken completely out of context - 'Or are we to say that we 
usually don't encounter such difficulties and things seem to work out

Hmmmmm... then, perhaps, the definition of fruit here is 'that which flies 
like a banana'.

>
>>  It may be intended one way and
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>Reified, I like that!

It can be a juicy concept out of which to make a mental snack; reification 
is the process by which an abstract is made to be considered as a concrete 
thing (res + faciere).

One can say 'this is a good table' and 'this is a good example'; 
'goodness' in such cases most frequently involves how the item in question 
fulfills the role of the object (table, example).  To speak of 'good' 
outside of an object (frequently encountered in the Platonic application 
of 'forms' or the 'eidos') is reification... what sense is to be made of 
this?

'This table partakes of the form/essence/eidos of The Good'.

>In considering my previous response, I examined
>the idea that 'meaning' is concrete and 'language' is abstract.

'Intention' may be considered concrete... 'I, as the speaker/author, wish 
to convey (x).'  Language is the vehicle and 'meaning' is, I would say, 
what happens when intention and interpretation match.

>Specifically, that meaning is something concrete that exists between
>the thing-for-others and the expression. In effect, vocabulary size and
>facility with language will affect how well the intent is conveyed.

Consider another Wittgensteinian example, the one of what he called 
'ultimate language', where two men are building a wall.  One holds out his 
hand and says 'Brick!'... and the other puts a brick in that hand.

>Instead, I chose to state 'a thing-for-others serves that purpose nicely'.

Things seem to work out alright, most of the time.

DD

Forms, Platonic {Gk. eide [eide]} 

The pure objects of mathematical and dialectical knowledge. In the 
vigorous realism of Plato's middle dialogues, necessary truths are taken 
to involve knowledge of eternal, unchanging Forms (or Ideas). Particular 
things in the realm of appearance are beautiful, or equal, or good only 
insofar as they participate in the universal Forms of Beauty, Equality, or 
the Good. The doctrine of Forms was attacked in Plato's own Parmenides and 
by Aristotle. 

--end quoted text

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: [OT} Wittgenstein and Meaning (was Re: Perfrom Thru)

_(thread depth: 55)_

- **From:** "Rick Smith" <ricksmith@mfi.net>
- **Date:** 2004-04-19T09:40:17-04:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<1087lm0ej1s6ve0@corp.supernews.com>`
- **References:** `<49f7d61d.0403142339.1c3c17df@posting.google.com> <107thkh60rere37@corp.supernews.com> <c5mmsl$at5$1@panix5.panix.com> <107vcj3119ke746@corp.supernews.com> <c5ojhh$aou$1@panix5.panix.com>`

```

<docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message news:c5ojhh$aou$1@panix5.panix.com...
> In article <107vcj3119ke746@corp.supernews.com>,
> Rick Smith <ricksmith@mfi.net> wrote:
> >
> ><docdwarf@panix.com> wrote in message
news:c5mmsl$at5$1@panix5.panix.com...
> >> In article <107thkh60rere37@corp.supernews.com>,
> >[snip]
> >> One can say 'this is a good table' and 'this is a good example';
> >> 'goodness' in such cases most frequently involves how the item in
question
> >> fulfills the role of the object (table, example).  To speak of 'good'
> >> outside of an object (frequently encountered in the Platonic
application
> >> of 'forms' or the 'eidos') is reification... what sense is to be made
of
> >> this?
> >>
…[23 more quoted lines elided]…
> --end quoted text

During the past three days, I have discovered that philosophy,
in general, has, for me, a sleep-inducing quality and finally
recalled that, about ten years ago, I re-read "Being and
Nothingness" for that very effect.

However, about three years ago, I visually scanned "Being
and Nothingness" searching for a particular quote that I did
not find. I was interested in the context. The quote is: 'It is not
mere caprice that causes us to do often and without annoyance
what would irritate us if commanded by another. That is
because the command and the prohibition causes us to
experience the others freedom across our own slavery.' I
suspect it may be a difference in translation. Nonetheless, I
did re-read a passage on Sadism that reminded me of the
US Congress.

While I found Sartre to be interesting, I found Flew's,
"A Dictionary of Philosophy", entry on Sir Karl Raimund
Popper to be facinating. What Popper provides is that
when experts and others make assertions, that are
'unrestricted generalizations', such assertions may be
'falsified'. Thus, if one can find an 'error of commission'
or, the more difficult to find, 'error of omission', one can,
by exposing the error, 'falsify' the assertion. In theory this
should be sufficient to alter the perception of others. But
when I exposed two such errors to a member of the
US Congress, a certain causal relationship was denied
by using a political definition of 'proximate cause' rather
the the legal definition. There was no response to the
exposure of the other error.

I have yet to present the description of the first error to any
expert. It is so controversial that I wanted the protection
of an intermediary. That is, I cannot force experts to review
their knowledge to confirm what I have found; but an
intermediary, in a position of civil authority, can deny further
subjection to the wrongful opinion of those experts until a
review is done.

Here I want to address the sleep-inducing quality of philosophy.
Flew's entry on Hegel contains the following: "... Kant argued
that the mind is not in a purely passive relation to its objects,
but contibutes much to them. Much, but not all; there are also
'things-in-themselves', that are wholly independent of any
mind, and contribute something to the objects that we know.
Things-in-themselves can be thought, but not known, in that
they are beyond the reach of experience, whereas the
knowledge of objects involves the possibility of experience.
Like Kant, Hegel was concerned with truths that are necessary,
but not necessary in the way that truths of formal logic are
necessary. But whereas Kant's main concern was with the
necessary truths presupposed by the natural sciences, Hegel
was more concerned with the necessary truths provided by
history, which he regarded as a process that follows necessary
laws. Hegel agreed with Kant that necessary truths must be
mind-imposed, but, like other critics of Kant, he rejected the
idea of the thing-in-itself as unintelligible. This led him to the
view that all that exists must be mental."

At the risk of over-simplification (and error), I offer the following
analogy from baseball. A thing-in-itself is like a pitch-in-itself;
that is, a ball in the pitcher's glove, an unrealized potential.
A thing-for-itself is like a pitch-for-itself; that is, the pitcher's
movement of the ball is a movement toward the realization of
pitch's potential. A thing-for-others is like a pitch-for-others; that
is, the realized potential, a balk, a wild pitch, a ball, a strike, a
pop-up, a line drive, a home run, whatever. Thus
things-in-themselves may be understood as the undiscovered
and things-for-others as that which is discovered. All discovery
and invention is the translation of a thing-in-itself to a
thing-for-others. From the above passage, Kant seems to have
recognized this about things-in-themselves; but this is not so of
Hegel. But my understanding comes from Sartre not Kant.
To the extent that recent philosophers have addressed the
same problems as the older and ancient philosophers and
provide a more complete and understandable explanation, the
study of the old and ancient is ... well ... boring.

Even Hegel's triads (thesis, antithesis, synthesis) cannot
compete with Popper's falsification as a means for advancing
knowledge. That is, how can one know what is rational in the
thesis and antithesis and, therefore, should appear in the
synthesis? I know of one such synthesis that failed to include
ethical considerations and therefore has resulted in persecution
that is still ongoing. (I am aware of the arguments, both thesis
and antithesis, and I will not discuss it further here because of
the ethical considerations.) What I am to learn from further
study of Hegel? That which is not to be believed?
```

Some might argue that using Sartre towards this end is nigh criminal...

... while Sartre himself argues that if one is willing to accept the 
punsihment one can commit any crime.

>innocent, argue the facts, if the client is guilty, argue the law.

I had heard it as 'When the law is in your favor then pound on the law.  
When the facts are in your favor then pound on the facts.  When neither is
in your favor then pound on the table.'

>
>The compiler vendor that compiles code just fine sometimes, but other 
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>How can it be syntax when it works sometimes?

It might be, Mr Zitzelberger, that interesting to look at the example that 
started this discussion.  The code was changed so that an 88'd field was 
expanded a few bytes; this resulted in the compiler generating too much 
code for a given segment and throwing back an error indicating a syntactic 
correction was needed, or a 'syntax correction'.

To treat this anthropomorphically... someone writes a paragraph and 
someone else reads it and interprets it according to the author's intent.  
The author then rewrites the same paragraph slightly, changing a few 
Anglo-Saxon based words for Latinate words which contain more letters.  
These words which have more letters now cause the reader's brain to fill 
up completely and so the reader returns the paragraph to the author, 
stating that it cannot be understood at all because it contains a syntax 
error.

There... doesn't that make sense?

DD
```

…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> 
I wouldn't touch them.

If you want to perform a series of paragraph, then presumably you can 
think of a logical name for them and make them into a section and 
perform that.

Perform thrus just make code more complex to debug as it's not obvious 
that paragraphs in the section can be jumped into.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Server Express 64bit and Oracle 9i problem (114) on AIX 5.2

- **From:** "Simon Tobias" <Simon.Tobias@nospam.microfocus.com>
- **Date:** 2004-04-01T10:53:58+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c4gobl$i8s$1@hyperion.microfocus.com>`
- **References:** `<ZYtac.99432$Kc3.3243636@twister2.libero.it> <c4eebn$acp$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <qAAac.103510$FJ6.3801037@twister1.libero.it>`

```
Hi Panos,

the script I provided is running fine for me using SX 4.0, and Oracle
9.2.0.5.

If you invoke the makefiles directly, while logged in under your Oracle user
ID thus :

cd $ORACLE_HOME/precomp/lib
make -f ins_precomp.mk rtsora

It will relink rtsora -- a 64-bit COBOL Run-Time -- which will end up in
your $ORACLE_HOME/bin directory.

I just tried that on my installation here, along with the command you're
attempting -- make LOC_RTSORA=newrts -f ins_precomp.mk newrts -- and both
are working ok for me, picking up libraries and objects from under
$ORACLE_HOME/lib (rather than lib32).

Which files are you seeing being reported as 32-bit files?

SimonT.
```

I use :

cob -t -g prog.cbl

to create a multi-threaded program. My cobol.dir file includes

confirm
p(cobsql)  csqlt=oracle8 sqldebug display verbose trace deb  end-c mode=ansi
endp

I then run the program using

cobrun_t prog.int

having first copied the rts I build on top of rts64_t

The same scenario worked from the first time using the 32bit product.


One thing I noticed today, is that the procob precompiler cannot be relinked
succesfully. When I try to run "relink precomp" to link all precompilers
according to Oracle instructions, I get an error when procob is reached,
mentioning a bad magic number. The strange part is that procob exists and
works, although it does not vaildate as a 64bit application using cobfile
(but anyway does it matter if procob is 32bit or 64bit since all it does is
to create cobol code with expanded sql statements...)

Panos

- **References:** `<ZYtac.99432$Kc3.3243636@twister2.libero.it> <c4eebn$acp$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <qAAac.103510$FJ6.3801037@twister1.libero.it> <c4gobl$i8s$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <86abc.106821$FJ6.3917960@twister1.libero.it> <c4jdvg$rvf$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <42ebc.103917$Kc3.3377921@twister2.libero.it>`

```
Hi Panos.

I think we'd be better discussing this on the Micro Focus forum, accessible
from  http://www.cobolportal.com . :-)

It sounds like the operating mode of COBOL -- i.e. 32-bit or 64-bit -- 
doesn't match your Oracle environment. On AIX, there are separate 32-bit and
64-bit products. If you're getting 'bad magic number' errors, it sounds like
your COBDIR/LIBPATH/PATH setting may be pointing to the wrong Server Express
product.

It *does* matter whether you use procob -- the 64-bit Pro*COBOL -- or
procob32, as they will generate different code, including the sizes of
variables passed into/from Oracle as pointers. If you use the wrong procob,
you will get RTS114 errors at execution time.

Cobsql will, from SX 2.2 SP1 onwards, support 64-bit Pro*COBOL. This ensures
that the correct procob binary gets called for your COBOL working mode, i.e.
32-bit or 64-bit.

As you've specified SQLDEBUG, you should see the command invoked by Cobsql.
Could you tell me what that is please? Also, when compiling your program, if
you pass the -v flag (verbose), please tell me what gets displayed.

Thanks.

Simon.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Server Express 64bit and Oracle 9i problem (114) on AIX 5.2

_(thread depth: 9)_

- **From:** "Simon Tobias" <Simon.Tobias@nospam.microfocus.com>
- **Date:** 2004-04-16T10:40:30+01:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c5o92b$7il$1@hyperion.microfocus.com>`
- **References:** `<ZYtac.99432$Kc3.3243636@twister2.libero.it> <c4eebn$acp$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <qAAac.103510$FJ6.3801037@twister1.libero.it> <c4gobl$i8s$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <86abc.106821$FJ6.3917960@twister1.libero.it> <c4jr2e$tb7$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <4316ba2eca2871f8555b223eee51d361@news.thenewsgroups.com>`

```
Hi Wayne (and Panos)

Apologies if you're aware of some of this already, but I just wanted to
clarify the steps involved in creating and executing a Pro*COBOL application
with Micro Focus Server Express on UNIX.

When installing Pro*COBOL on UNIX (as part of the main Oracle installation),
you need to have your COBOL environment setup, in order for the installer to
relink a COBOL RTS containing the Oracle support libraries
(rtsora/rtsora32/rtsora64).

The 64-bit edition of Oracle 9i on AIX 5.x creates rtsora -- the 64-bit
version of the run-time -- and rtsora32 -- the 32-bit version of the
run-time.

It's imperative that you use the correct edition of Server Express, i.e.
32-bit or 64-bit -- note well, that these are separate products on this
platform -- for the mode in which you wish to use Oracle. In addition, you
need to ensure that LIBPATH is set to point to the correct Oracle 'lib'
directory -- $ORACLE_HOME/lib32 for 32-bit, or $ORACLE_HOME/lib for 64-bit

If you wish to recreate those executables, say if you've updated your COBOL
environment since installing Oracle, then from looking at the makefiles -- 
ins_precomp.mk and env_precomp.mk -- then the effective commands to use to
re-link the run-time correctly are as follows (logged in under your Oracle
user ID) :

either mode:
<set up COBDIR, ORACLE_HOME, ORACLE_BASE, ORACLE_SID as appropriate for your
installation>
export PATH=$COBDIR/bin:$ORACLE_HOME/bin:$PATH

32-bit :
export LIBPATH=$COBDIR/lib:$ORACLE_HOME/lib32:$LIBPATH
cd $ORACLE_HOME/precomp/lib
make LIBDIR=lib32 -f ins_precomp.mk EXE=rtsora32 rtsora32

64-bit:
export LIBPATH=$COBDIR/lib:$ORACLE_HOME/lib:$LIBPATH
cd $ORACLE_HOME/precomp/lib
make -f ins_precomp.mk rtsora

Regarding precompiling your application, Oracle provide two versions of
Pro*COBOL. Again, you need to use the correct one depending on whether
you're creating a 32-bit or 64-bit application, as the precompiler will
generate different code.

If invoking Pro*COBOL directly, you need to use :

32-bit : procob32 / procob18_32 , e.g.
procob32 myapp.pco
cob -it myapp.cob
rtsora32 myapp.int

or
64-bit : procob / procob18 , e.g.
procob myapp.pco
cob -it myapp.cob
rtsora myapp.int

If you're using Server Express 2.2 SP1 or later, you can also compile using
the Cobsql preprocessor, which will invoke the correct version of Pro*COBOL
under the covers, allowing for a single precompile-compile step, e.g.

cob -ik myapp.pco -C "p(cobsql) csqlt==oracle8 endp"

This method also aids debugging, as you will see the original source code
while animating, rather than the output from the precompiler. See the Server
Express Database Access manual. Prior to SX 2.2 SP1, Cobsql only supported
the creation of 32-bit applications.

I hope this helps -- if you're still having problems, please let me know.

Regards,
SimonT.
```

```
Hi Wayne.

On the surface, it appears as if you're not picking up the correct Pro*COBOL
binary.

If you invoke 'procob' from the command line, you should see something along
the lines of :

Pro*COBOL: Release 9.2.0.4.0 - Production on Mon Apr 19 13:38:07 2004

followed by a list of Pro*COBOL options.

Do you see this, or do you see a different banner (say, Pro*ADA, or
Pro*Fortran)? Assuming you see something other than a Pro*COBOL banner, then
if you invoke 'whence procob', does it show procob as being picked up from
your Oracle bin directory (/home/oracle/9.2.0/bin/procob in my case) ?

If you're either not seeing the correct Pro*COBOL banner, or it's not
located in the correct directory, I'd suggest rebuilding the procob and
procob32 binaries. Logged in under your Oracle user ID, with the Oracle
environment set up :

cd $ORACLE_HOME/precomp/lib
make -f ins_precomp.mk procob32 procob

and then try your compilation process again.

Regards,
SimonT.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Server Express 64bit and Oracle 9i problem (114) on AIX 5.2

_(thread depth: 12)_

- **From:** sprog <sprog.151jzy@mail.codecomments.com>
- **Date:** 2004-04-21T01:07:27+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ecc27634a2cd8c14215491fd2dfaa183@news.thenewsgroups.com>`
- **References:** `<ZYtac.99432$Kc3.3243636@twister2.libero.it> <c4eebn$acp$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <qAAac.103510$FJ6.3801037@twister1.libero.it> <c4gobl$i8s$1@hyperion.microfocus.com> <86abc.106821$FJ6.3917960@twister1.libero.it> <c60gv9$3j9$1@hyperion.microfocus.com>`

```
Hi Simon

Firstly, thanks for all your help, it was greatly appreciated.

We have the solution to our problem:

The problem is resolved by modifying the line in the job from:

	make -f $SRC_DIR/procob.mk COBS="$SRC_DIR/PFEM025A.cob SYSDATE.cob
CNTLGET.cob" EXE=$SRC_DIR/PFEM025A
to
	make -f $SRC_DIR/procob.mk build COBS="$SRC_DIR/PFEM025A.cob
SYSDATE.cob CNTLGET.cob" EXE=$SRC_DIR/PFEM025A

It appears this (build keyword) is not a requirement for the job to run
on Solaris but is for AIX.

All is working fine.

Cheers

Wayne

> *Hi Wayne.
> 
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
> SimonT. *

_Thread started [2004-03](2004-03.md#t-bbc61b389e)._

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** chris.glazier@microfocus.com (Chris Glazier)
- **Date:** 2004-04-01T06:07:10-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<260cd566.0404010607.d86eb24@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<e66a875b.0403201400.5c5ee637@posting.google.com> <b3638c46.0403210635.59a62084@posting.google.com> <e66a875b.0403241109.17adf59b@posting.google.com> <260cd566.0403291043.78724c70@posting.google.com> <e66a875b.0403300941.1a15b541@posting.google.com>`

```
orzo617@hotmail.com (sb) wrote in message news:<e66a875b.0403300941.1a15b541@posting.google.com>...
> chris.glazier@microfocus.com (Chris Glazier) wrote in message news:<260cd566.0403291043.78724c70@posting.google.com>...
> > orzo617@hotmail.com (sb) wrote in message news:<e66a875b.0403241109.17adf59b@posting.google.com>...
…[173 more quoted lines elided]…
> SB

Just to close out this thread, the problem was occuring because the
other .DLLS that were being called by the main COBOL .DLL were NOT
compiled with the REENTRANT"2" directive.

When creating a multi-threaded Net Express COBOL application, then ALL
of the programs within the application need to be compiled with one of
the multi-threaded directives, "SERIAL", REENTRANT"1" or REENTRANT"2".

It is not enough just to link these programs with the multi-threaded
run-time system.

There were also a few matters of CALL-CONVENTION from VB to COBOL and
some .ASP/IIS settings that had to be changed since this application
was not running under COM+, but instead it was handling database
transactions through COMMIT/ROLLBACK statements within the COBOL
programs.

It seems to working fine now...
```

brnetg@yahoo.com (Brent) wrote 

> > > I am a student and am having troubles reading in two .dat files in
> > > order to compare them.  The first file contains a student name and
> > > test answers ex:(John Doe     ADCBAADDCC).  The other file contains
> > > the answer key ex:(ADDBDCDCDC).  

> OK, here is my complied code.  I can read in the name and answers but
> i can't figure out how to compare each individual answer with the ones
> in the key(LAB5ANSW.dat").

You put them into 'paired array', which is a pair (two) tables holding
the answer key (PIC X OCCURS 10) and the students answers alongside
each other and for each answer (PERFORM VARYING .. UNTIL .. ) counting
(ADD 1) which ones match (IF .. = .. ):

              A D D B D C D C D C
              A D C B A A D D C C
              1 2   3     4     5

 
>        ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
>        INPUT-OUTPUT SECTION.
>        FILE-CONTROL.
>            SELECT CUSTMAST ASSIGN TO

You have obviously copied another program that was completely
unrelated to the problem.  Having 'CUSTMAST' (Customer Master) and
'SALESRPT' (Sales Report) does not help understand the probem and
disguises the solution.  Taking another program and mindlessly
changing things that you recognise does not help you learn the process
of solving problems.

>        0000-MAINLINE.
>            PERFORM 010-INITIALIZE.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>            GOBACK.

This is the structure that they used to teach 40 years ago.  The
paragraph numbers (010- 300- 099-) really useful in finding the code
in the boxes of punched cards that was the source code deck and in the
printed lineflow listings.  If you are going to do this then at least
make them useful and keep them in sequence.

To get a sensible solution you need to analyse the problem and break
it down into parts.

The probem is:

          read answer key
          for each student set of answers:
              read stuent answer
              compares answers to key
              print count of correct answers

First you should be able to define the files, after all this is what
has been given in the question.  Using _meaningful_ names:

        ENVIRONMENT DIVISION.
        FILE-CONTROL.
            SELECT Answer-File 
               ASSIGN TO "C:\Lab5\LAB5ANSW.DAT"
               ORGANIZATION IS LINE SEQUENTIAL.
           SELECT Test-File
               ASSIGN TO "C:\Lab5\LAB5TEST.DAT"
               ORGANIZATION IS LINE SEQUENTIAL.
           SELECT Result-File 
               ASSIGN TO "C:\Lab5\LAB5RPT.TXT"
               ORGANIZATION IS LINE SEQUENTIAL.
       DATA DIVISION.
       FILE SECTION.
       FD  Answer-File.
       01  Answer-Record            PIC X(10).

       FD  Test-File.
       01  Test-Record.
           03  Test-Name            PIC X(15).
           03  Test-Answers         PIC X(10).     

       FD  Result-File.
       01  Result-Record.
           03  Result-Name          PIC X(15).
           03  Result-Count         PIC ZZ9.     

There will need to be some working areas including the 'paired
arrays':
       WORKING-STORAGE SECTION.

       01  Answer-Key.
           03  Correct-Answer       PIC X OCCURS 10.
       01  Answer-Compare.
           03  Student-Answer       PIC X OCCURS 10.

       01  Step                     PIC S9(4).
       01  Answers-Correct          PIC S9(4).

       PROCEDURE DIVISION.
       Process-Students.

           PERFORM Read-Answer-Key           
           PERFORM Process-Student-Answers
           STOP RUN
           .

       Read-Answer-Key.

           OPEN INPUT Answer-File
       *   you should be checking if the open was successful here
       *   but the run-time will give an error if it isn't.
           READ Answer-File  
               INTO Answer-Key
           CLOSE Answer-File
           .

        Process-Student-Answers.
           OPEN INPUT Test-File
           OPEN OUTPUT Result-File

           PERFORM
               UNTIL Test-Name = HIGH-VALUES

               READ Test-File
                   AT END 
                       MOVE HIGH-VALUES TO Test-Record
                   NOT AT END
                        
                       MOVE Test-Answers    TO Answer-Compare
                       PERFORM Count-Answers-Correct

                       MOVE Test-Name       TO Result-Name
                       MOVE Answers-Correct TO Result-Count
                       WRITE Result-Record
               END-READ
           END-PERFORM

           CLOSE Test-File
           CLOSE Result-File
           .
      Count-Answers-Correct.

           MOVE ZERO      TO Answers-Correct
           PERFORM
               VARYING Step FROM 1 BY 1
                 UNTIL Step > 10

               IF ( Student-Answer(Step) = Correct-Answer(Step) )
                   ADD 1    TO Answers-Correct
               END-IF
           END-PERFORM
           .

Of course if you just submit this as the answer the tutor will notice
that it is not your work.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Compare two .dat files

_(thread depth: 4)_

- **From:** "Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net>

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<c4htg3$1oe$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`
- **References:** `<ad38a8cd.0403290932.43c01f87@posting.google.com> <106hji331h16l53@corp.supernews.com> <ad38a8cd.0403312029.3bda3f37@posting.google.com> <217e491a.0404011154.299106e@posting.google.com>`

```

On  1-Apr-2004, riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard) wrote:

> >        0000-MAINLINE.
> >            PERFORM 010-INITIALIZE.
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> make them useful and keep them in sequence.

Without being as useful these days, there still can be something said for using
this type of paragraph prefix:

1.  A shop can have a standard similar to this:
     0000-MAIN.
            PERFORM 0100-INIT.
            PERFORM 1000-PROCESS-LOOP UNTIL SW-END.
            PERFORM 9000-FINALE.
            GOBACK.
 ...
     7000-READ.
...
     8000-WRITE.

As you imply, don't just stick a number in front - do so for a purpose.   We get
some idea where particular code will be found from program to program.

I also like being able to in TSO type
F p'#' 8 prev
to find the previous paragraph name without picking up a comment.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Compare two .dat files

_(thread depth: 5)_

- **From:** riplin@Azonic.co.nz (Richard)
- **Date:** 2004-04-02T17:42:34-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<217e491a.0404021742.59ec6492@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<ad38a8cd.0403290932.43c01f87@posting.google.com> <106hji331h16l53@corp.supernews.com> <ad38a8cd.0403312029.3bda3f37@posting.google.com> <217e491a.0404011154.299106e@posting.google.com> <c4htg3$1oe$1@peabody.colorado.edu>`

```
"Howard Brazee" <howard@brazee.net> wrote 

> > This is the structure that they used to teach 40 years ago.  The
> > paragraph numbers (010- 300- 099-) really useful in finding the code
> > in the boxes of punched cards that was the source code deck and in the
> > printed lineflow listings. 
 
> I also like being able to in TSO type
> F p'#' 8 prev
> to find the previous paragraph name without picking up a comment.

Given that TSO is only marginally more recent technology than card
packs, then yes, paragraph numbering may well be useful there too  
;-)

>1.  A shop can have a standard similar to this:
>     0000-MAIN.
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>     8000-WRITE.

Granted, if you are rewriting the same program over and over again, as
batch systems tend to be, then it makes sense to have the same
paragraphs names they have had for the last 40 years.

In general the number is used like a page number in the lineflow
listing.  Finding a '3201-name' one knows whether to flick the pages
forwards or backwards.

For the last 20 years I have used editors that I just put the cursor
over the name and hit a key and it goes to that point without me
knowing or caring where it might be in a printed listing.

To me, the idea that paragraphs must be in some hierarchical
structural _and_ logical order, and must be coded as such, destroys
the concept of reusability and imposes constraints on structural
decomposition that is entirely unrequired.

Paragraph numbering is, after all, a solution to problems that existed
40 years ago, and was appropriate to the types of programs written
then.  My programs do not work in such a simplistic manner that a
hierarchy represents.  It is the user that chooses which part of the
code is to run at any particular time, numbering schemes make no sense
and do nothing for finding or understanding.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Compare two .dat files

- **From:** epc8@juno.com (E P Chandler)
- **Date:** 2004-04-01T20:11:32-08:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7f64e2ff.0404012011.3e1e0d8a@posting.google.com>`
- **References:** `<ad38a8cd.0403290932.43c01f87@posting.google.com> <106hji331h16l53@corp.supernews.com> <ad38a8cd.0403312029.3bda3f37@posting.google.com>`

```
> > Brent wrote:
> > > I am a student and am having troubles reading in two .dat files in
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> > 

[snip]

 
> OK, here is my complied code.  I can read in the name and answers but
> i can't figure out how to compare each individual answer with the ones
…[30 more quoted lines elided]…
>       *

[snip]

It would help if your variable and file names matched the domain of
the problem you are trying to solve. It would make it easier for
others to look at your program and help you. This problem has nothing
to do with updating a master file.

When posting a code sample and asking for help, pare the program down
to the minimum that does the job and exercises the actual problem.

Before writing a program, write some pseudo code, then expand it out
into actual programming statements. Yes, I do use code skeletons, but
they are the bare bones minimums that let me see what I am doing in
solving a problem.
Add the details (like report formatting, header lines, page numbers)
later.

Now that I'm done preaching, let's get to your specific problem. You
only need to read the file of correct answers *once*. Then for each
student, read the student name and answers. Total the correct answers,
and write a report line.

Instead of reading records into one field which is a fixed length
string, why not read directly into an array (table)?

For example, in your FDs use something like:

01 ANSWER-KEY.
  02 KEY-ANSWER PIC X OCCURS 20 TIMES.

01 STUDENT-RECORD.
  02 STUDENT-NAME PIC X(20).
  02 STUDENT-ANSWERS.
    04 STUDENT-ANSWER PIC X OCCURS 20 TIMES.

Then just compare STUDENT-ANSWER(index-variable) to
KEY-ANSWER(index-variable).
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Compare two .dat files

```
Brent wrote:
> OK, here is my complied code.  I can read in the name and answers but
> i can't figure out how to compare each individual answer with the ones
> in the key(LAB5ANSW.dat").

I concur with Richard's evaluation - naming files meaningful things 
really helps keep things straight in your mind.  In fact, it may be this 
that has caused some of the problems you're having.

That aside, if your instructor has told you to prefix your paragraph 
names with numbers, go ahead and do that - you wouldn't want to fail 
just out of style.  However, as Richard said, there is no requirement 
within COBOL to make that so.

(As it happens, the shop in which I'm currently working has a standard 
of prefixing numbers of at least three digits in all main program 
paragraphs - so, you may not get away from that.  I'll stay out of the 
"which is better" debate - but, suffice it to say, if you do use 
numbers, try to group them logically and make sure they're always in 
sequence.  It makes things a lot easier for the next person who comes 
behind you.)

Richard gave you enough information to complete this particular 
assignment, but I encourage you to take his solution and look into it - 
if you don't understand what a particular line or section of code does, 
look up those verbs in your textbook or compiler language reference 
manual (LRM).  If you're still confused, ask - we here in this newsgroup 
don't mind explaining things if we can sense that the person asking is 
trying too.  :)
```

```
Hello Brent,

I am glad to see that you have made a start, however there are as
might be expected some faults, at least some of which I shall comment
on in the text.  Overall it is quite a tidy program, obviously
cannibalised from an earlier exercise.  Such cloning is commendable,
but for clarity and general comprehension it is desirable to ensure
that your new data and procedure names are relevant to the function of
the current program.

brnetg@yahoo.com (Brent) wrote in message news:<ad38a8cd.0403312029.3bda3f37@posting.google.com>...
> LX-i <lxi0007@netscape.net> wrote in message news:<106hji331h16l53@corp.supernews.com>...
> > Brent wrote:
…[72 more quoted lines elided]…
>
define STUDENT-GRADES as a table (aka array) e.g.

             05  STUDENT-GRADES          PIC X         OCCURS 20.
 
>        01  ANSWER-MASTER-RECORD.
>            05  ANSWER-KEY              PIC X(20).

define the answers as a table similar to student grades e.g.

             05  ANSWER-DETAIL           PIC X         OCCURS 20.

For comprehension, it is preferable not to use the term KEY as part of
a data name unless it is a file key or table key or some other case
where the term is appropriate.
>       *
>        01  PRINT-AREA                  PIC X(80).
…[11 more quoted lines elided]…
>       *    05  CHANGE-AMOUNT           PIC S9(7)V99.

reinstate the group item "CALCULATED-FIELDS but instead of
CHANGE-AMOUNt use a meaningful data name such as "CORRECT ANSWERS"
with a PIC S999.
>       *
>        01  PRINT-FIELDS        PACKED-DECIMAL.
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>            05  SPACE-CONTROL   PIC S9.

A more reasonable value for LINES-ON-PAGE would be 50>60
>       *
>       *
…[51 more quoted lines elided]…
>            05  CL-STUDENT-GRADES    PIC X(20).

replace the Picture for CL-STUDENT-GRADES with a usage display numeric
picture.
large enough to contain the value from CORRECT-ANSWERS.

>            05  FILLER               PIC X(16)      VALUE SPACE.
> 
…[40 more quoted lines elided]…
>                 END-EVALUATE.

It is always a good idea to have the scope terminator of a statement,
in this case END-EVALUATE in the same column as its statement.  In
this program, the EVALUATE is unnecessary.  Instead you need a perform
statement that cycles through the current student record's answers
checking them against the corresponding correct answers to tally the
number of correct answers in CORRECT-ANSWERS.

>            PERFORM 310-READ-CUSTOMER-RECORD.
> 
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
>            MOVE STUDENT-GRADES       TO CL-STUDENT-GRADES.

Instead of STUDENT-GRADES move CORRECT-ANSWERS

>            MOVE CUSTOMER-LINE TO PRINT-AREA.
>            PERFORM 850-WRITE-REPORT-LINE.
…[37 more quoted lines elided]…
> John Doe        16

Good luck, Robert

"ShadowFox" <me@email.com> wrote in message
news:NYobc.165326$Cb.1659753@attbi_s51...
> Guy's
 -10   Improper use of possesive form; plural form required.
- 2    Comma missing following salutation

>if they can't write in English dont'
-6  apostrophe misplaced in contraction

>respond to the post. I have lost many jobs to the non usa programmers
 -5 "USA" is an acronym and should be capitalized
-4  should use hyphen between 'non' and 'USA'
-8  'non-USA'  is not required adjective form
-10 singular article 'the'  used when referring to plural ('programmers')

>who don't speak the language and can't read the code let's not help them
stick it to us
-10  End of phrase or sentence following 'word 'code' not terminated by
semicolon or period and start of new sentence

> rememmber if the transistor was a national secret the transistor radio
would
-6 spelling error ('remember')

> have been made in the good ole U.S.A.
- colloquial form 'ole' inappropriate here (warning only).

Thank you for your timely reminder to be on the lookout for those lacking
skills in written English.

MCM

>Subject: Re: IF they can't write english don't answer
>From: SkippyPB swiegand@neo.rr.NOSPAM.com 
…[41 more quoted lines elided]…
>helping "them ferigners" out ;)

I don't understand the point you are trying to make.  
Are you saying: 
(1) Off-shoring does not exist?
(2) No U.S. jobs have been lost to off-shoring?
(3) Those U.S. citizens who remain employed in I.T. have done so because of
    their massively superior intelligence and diligence? 
(4) Every U.S. citizen who has been fired from an I.T. job has been so because
    of his obviously inferior intelligence or diligence?
(5) Any statement which could have emanated from racism should immediately
    be angrily rejected, based on that possibility, without regard to any truth
it 
    might contain?  

If you agree with any of those points, you are not thinking clearly.  
The facts are clear: 
U.S. companies are moving all I.T. jobs off-shore as fast they are able.  
Not all the Indians are smarter, but they are all cheaper.  Not all the
Americans are stupider, but they are all more expensive.
The motivation is simple economics--it has absolutely nothing to do
with racism or patriotism, and it has little to do with competency. 

Perhaps you meant to say that any person who cannot write with correct
spelling and punctuation should be ignored?  (Excluding, of course, those 
who acquired their deficiency by accident of birth.)  I think that criterion
would eliminate 90 percent of everyone in I.T.  

People who have held onto their jobs--primarily because they are
self-employed or because their employer does not have enough
know-how to find a suitable replacement overseas--invariably believe
that they have kept their jobs because they are brilliant.  This is not
true.  No matter how brilliant you are, there are, let me assure you,
at least ten people more brilliant than you among the two or three billion 
people in Asia.  You guys are like turkeys in the turkey farm that
go around high-fiving each other when the axe chops off some
other turkey's head for Thanksgiving dinner, supposing that they
have cleverly outwitted the turkey farmer.  

Instead of English critiques, please address the point raised:  
American I.T. jobs are being lost to India, Pakistan, and China.  This
situation is not good for the individuals who lose their jobs.  Sooner
or later, it is not good for the governments who live off the taxes
paid by those individuals.  

P.S. Your "possesive" does not poses enough "s"'s.


Ross
Vontay, Virginia
http://community.webshots.com/user/ross_klatte
http://www.geocities.com/foundlingfather/
```

to that I have waited/cooked/tended bar, worked in a factory, drove long 
haul trucks, stevedored and soldiered.

Manufacturing would have been fine if my alternative was scratching a 
living out of dirt (but agriculture is another topic).  But with other  
options available, it was the bottom of the sewer.

world countries that received those jobs manage to use them to boost 
their collective living conditions out of subsistence farming and then 
into so-called "knowledge jobs" -- as India has in the last generation.

for better, happier pursuits -- but time and infrastructure are needed 
for that, the industrial revolution is still young in many parts of the 
world.

Mike wrote:
>> If you look at the other side: that other nations outsource to the
>> USA
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Russia or even Europe to the USA.

Here's one. I had a programmer doing COBOL development work telecommuting
from Israel . I recently replaced him with a local (although, strictly
speaking, Israel is not India, China, Russia, or Europe). But look at the
big picture:

First, in the past few years, foreign-owned companies have hired almost ten
times as many workers in the U.S. as American companies have hired abroad
(1.8 million vs 200,000 were the last figures I saw).

Second, labor, like anything else, is an ingredient in every product or
service. Any company, domestic or foreign, owes a duty to its stockholders,
its customers, and its employees to produce the best product it can. By
buying "raw materials" at the best possible price, it furthers that goal.

ALL forms of protectionism are evil, be they tariffs on goods and raw
materials or restrictions on the use of labor.

This IS a Republican mantra, but that does not diminish its truth.



>
>> There is always some discomfort for individuals -- just imagine how
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
> retrained jobs outsourced again.

Nonsense. Manufacturing output in the U.S. has increased every year back
into the dim past. We just make different things (and more of them). There
are fewer people employed in manufacturing and by every objective standard,
that's good.

>
> Would you like fries with that retooled job?

Interestingly, the company with the largest revenue in the U.S. is not a
manufacturer. And, no, its not a bank, an insurance or financial company, a
health provider, a law firm, part of transportation, or involved in
agriculture - just to name a few huge industries that don't MAKE something.

I used the label 'bad business' in order to maintain symmetry... see

>
>It might have gotten a reputation for being 'bad business' because of 
>the behavior of the various unions of the last century.  The act of 
>tring to get maximum value for your work hour is just plain honest  
>capitolism.

I have no idea how unions might or might not be made to fit into this 
scenario... what I have seen might be described this way.

Businessperson: 'I always try to get the most amount of (commodity) for 
the least amount of money... I give *just barely* enough to get what I 
want and I always want more.'

General Wisdom: 'This is a good attitude towards business.'

Employee: 'I always try to give the least amount of work (commodity) for 
the most amount of money... I give *just barely* enough to get what I 
want and I always want more.'

General Wisdom: 'This is a bad attitude towards business.'

DD
